Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
12 min
General Liability for Road Hazards
Rulings on liability for death/injury from objects placed in the road

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule for liability when a person places a hazard in a public road, identify the exceptions (permission, intentional crossing, normal clothing), and apply the rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
A road hazard causing death places diyah on the causer's 'āqilah, unless the victim intentionally crosses or the causer had permission.

This lesson covers the Hanafi rulings on civil liability for unintended harm caused by objects placed in public thoroughfares. The foundational principle is that if someone dies from a hazard (e.g., a water spout, a well, a stone), the blood money (diyah) is wajib upon the 'āqilah of the person who created the hazard. However, liability is affected by factors such as the victim's intentional act, the presence of permission from the imam, and the nature of the object (e.g., normal clothing).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Identify the act: placing a hazard in the road (e.g., digging a well, placing a stone, installing a drain) causing death. The ruling is that diyah is on the 'āqilah.
The text explicitly states 'fadiyatuhu 'ala 'aqilatihi'.
2
Locate the dalil
The text proves this by analogy (qiyas) to the established cases of digging a well or placing a stone, which are from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
The phrase 'as if he dug a well' indicates the qiyas.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The common illa is tasbīb (indirect causation) without direct intent. The analogy holds because both cases involve setting up a condition leading to harm.
Qiyas jaliy, as the illa is clear.
4
Note the exceptions and conditions
Liability is removed if the victim intentionally passes (e.g., blind or at night) and the hazard was placed with permission. Also, carrying objects: liable unless it is a garment being worn (umum al-balwa).
Intentional act of victim (mubasharah) overrules tasbīb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق فإن مات أحد بسقوطها فديته على عاقلته كما لو حفر بئرا في طريق أو وضع حجرا فتلف به إنسان ولو بهيمة فضمانها في ماله ومن جعل بالوعة في طريق بأمر سلطان أو في ملكه أو وضع خشبة فيها أو قنطرة بلا إذن الإمام فتعمد رجل المرور عليها لم يضمن وأما إذا لم يتعمد المرور بأن كان أعمى أو مر ليلا يضمن إذا وضعه بغير إذن الإمام فأما إذا وضعه بإذن الإمام فلا يضمن وكذا إذا حفر في الطريق بغير إذن الإمام فسقط ومات يضمن ومن حمل شيئا في الطريق فسقط على إنسان ضمن ولو كان رداء قد لبسه فسقط لا يضمن ومسجد لعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم قنديلا أو جعل فيه بواري أو حصاة فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان من غيرهم ضمن وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله وقالا لا يضمن في الوجهين وإن جلس فيه رجل منهم فعطب به أحد ضمن إن كان في غير الصلاة وإن كان فيها لا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن على كل حال وذكر صدر الإسلام أن الأظهر ما قالاه
What a man causes in the road: if someone dies from its fall, his blood money is on his 'aqilah, just as if he dug a well in the road or placed a stone and a person perished from it. And if it is an animal, its liability is from his own wealth. And whoever places a drain in the road by order of the sultan, or in his own property, or places a plank in it, or an archway without the imam's permission, if a man intentionally crosses upon it, he is not liable. But if he did not intentionally cross, such as if he was blind or passed at night, he is liable if he placed it without the imam's permission. If he placed it with permission, he is not liable. Likewise, if he dug in the road without the imam's permission and someone fell and died, he is liable. And whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person, he is liable. But if it was a garment he was wearing and it fell, he is not liable. And a mosque belonging to a clan: if a man from them hangs a lantern, or spreads mats or pebbles in it, and a person is injured by it, he is not liable. But if it is someone from outside, he is liable. This is according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not liable in both cases. And if a man from them sits in it and someone is injured by his sitting, he is liable if it was not during prayer, but if during prayer, not liable according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: he is not liable in all cases. And Sadr al-Islam said the apparent view is what the two said.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a series of rulings on civil liability (daman) for unintended harm caused by actions in public spaces. The section is termed 'ma yuhdithuhu al-rajul fi al-tariq' (what a man causes in the road). The first ruling is the foundational principle: if someone dies from a hazard a person places in the road—like a water spout, gutter, or similar projection—the blood money (diyah) is wajib upon the 'aqilah (agnatic tribe) of the person who created the hazard. This is proven by qiyas to the established cases of digging a well or placing a stone, which are from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The ruling distinguishes between human and animal victims: for humans, the 'aqilah bears the diyah; for animals, the liability is from the causer's own wealth because 'aqilah only covers human life. The text then details exceptions: if the hazard is placed with the sultan's permission or on one's own property, or if the passerby intentionally encounters it, the causer is not liable because the victim's intentional act severs the causal link (mubasharah overcomes tasabbub). However, if the passerby is blind or passes at night, the causer is liable if he acted without permission. The second ruling covers carrying objects: if a person carries something and it falls and kills someone, he is liable, unless it is a garment he is wearing—then no liability because he has no intention to secure it and the need is general (umum al-balwa). The third ruling addresses actions in a clan's mosque: hanging a lantern or spreading mats. Abu Hanifa holds that a member of the clan is not liable because they have authority over the mosque, but a non-member is liable. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that neither is liable because it is an act of worship (qurbah). The fourth ruling is about sitting in the mosque: if a person sits and causes injury, Abu Hanifa distinguishes between sitting for prayer (no liability) and other activities (liability); the two say no liability in all cases because sitting is necessary for prayer. Later authorities (Sadr al-Islam) prefer the view of the two, and the fatwa is upon that.

Key Terms
عاقلة (al-āqilah): The male agnatic relatives of a person who accidentally caused death, who bear the diyah (blood money) collectively.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Indirect causation, where a person sets up a condition that leads to harm without directly inflicting it.
مباشرة (mubāsharah): Direct act causing harm, which overrides indirect causation in liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who bears the diyah if a person dies from falling into a well dug in the road by someone else?
A) The person who dug the well
B) The 'aqilah of the person who dug the well
C) The state treasury
D) The victim's family

Key Takeaway
A road hazard causing death places diyah on the causer's 'āqilah, but liability is removed if the victim intentionally crosses or the hazard was placed with permission, and normal clothing is exempt due to general need.

Liability for Hazards in a Clan Mosque: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mosque liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on liability for hazards in a clan mosque and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
In a clan mosque, a member is not liable for injuries from hanging lanterns or sitting during prayer; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) extend this to all cases, and their view is preferred.

This lesson examines a specific area of liability: actions taken within a clan mosque (masjid al-‘ashīrah). Abu Hanifa distinguishes between members and non-members, and between prayer and non-prayer contexts. The two (Sahibayn) hold that there is no liability in all these cases because the actions are acts of worship (qurbah). Later Hanafi authorities prefer the view of the two, and it is the operative position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
A man from a clan (or outside) hangs a lantern, spreads mats, or sits in the clan mosque, and someone is injured.
The mosque is specific to a clan ('ashirah).
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
If a member hangs a lantern or spreads mats, he is not liable. But if a non-member does so, he is liable. For sitting: liable if not in prayer, not liable if during prayer.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on membership and context.
3
Identify the two's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say there is no liability in all cases, regardless of membership or prayer.
Their reasoning: these are acts of worship, so liability is lifted.
4
Follow the tarjih
Sadr al-Islam and later authorities (al-'Inayah, al-Dhakhirah) prefer the view of the two, and it is the mufta bihi.
The weight of later scholarship supports the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق فإن مات أحد بسقوطها فديته على عاقلته كما لو حفر بئرا في طريق أو وضع حجرا فتلف به إنسان ولو بهيمة فضمانها في ماله ومن جعل بالوعة في طريق بأمر سلطان أو في ملكه أو وضع خشبة فيها أو قنطرة بلا إذن الإمام فتعمد رجل المرور عليها لم يضمن وأما إذا لم يتعمد المرور بأن كان أعمى أو مر ليلا يضمن إذا وضعه بغير إذن الإمام فأما إذا وضعه بإذن الإمام فلا يضمن وكذا إذا حفر في الطريق بغير إذن الإمام فسقط ومات يضمن ومن حمل شيئا في الطريق فسقط على إنسان ضمن ولو كان رداء قد لبسه فسقط لا يضمن ومسجد لعشيرة فعلق رجل منهم قنديلا أو جعل فيه بواري أو حصاة فعطب به رجل لم يضمن وإن كان من غيرهم ضمن وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله وقالا لا يضمن في الوجهين وإن جلس فيه رجل منهم فعطب به أحد ضمن إن كان في غير الصلاة وإن كان فيها لا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا لا يضمن على كل حال وذكر صدر الإسلام أن الأظهر ما قالاه
What a man causes in the road: if someone dies from its fall, his blood money is on his 'aqilah, just as if he dug a well in the road or placed a stone and a person perished from it. And if it is an animal, its liability is from his own wealth. And whoever places a drain in the road by order of the sultan, or in his own property, or places a plank in it, or an archway without the imam's permission, if a man intentionally crosses upon it, he is not liable. But if he did not intentionally cross, such as if he was blind or passed at night, he is liable if he placed it without the imam's permission. If he placed it with permission, he is not liable. Likewise, if he dug in the road without the imam's permission and someone fell and died, he is liable. And whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person, he is liable. But if it was a garment he was wearing and it fell, he is not liable. Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a series of rulings on civil liability (daman) for unintended harm caused by actions in public spaces. The section is termed 'ma yuhdithuhu al-rajul fi al-tariq' (what a man causes in the road). The first ruling is the foundational principle: if someone dies from a hazard a person places in the road—like a water spout, gutter, or similar projection—the blood money (diyah) is wajib upon the 'aqilah (agnatic tribe) of the person who created the hazard. This is proven by qiyas to the established cases of digging a well or placing a stone, which are from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The ruling distinguishes between human and animal victims: for humans, the 'aqilah bears the diyah; for animals, the liability is from the causer's own wealth because 'aqilah only covers human life. The text then details exceptions: if the hazard is placed with the sultan's permission or on one's own property, or if the passerby intentionally encounters it, the causer is not liable because the victim's intentional act severs the causal link (mubasharah overcomes tasabbub). However, if the passerby is blind or passes at night, the causer is liable if he acted without permission. The second ruling covers carrying objects: if a person carries something and it falls and kills someone, he is liable, unless it is a garment he is wearing—then no liability because he has no intention to secure it and the need is general (umum al-balwa). The third ruling addresses actions in a clan's mosque: hanging a lantern or spreading mats. Abu Hanifa holds that a member of the clan is not liable because they have authority over the mosque, but a non-member is liable. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that neither is liable because it is an act of worship (qurbah). The fourth ruling is about sitting in the mosque: if a person sits and causes injury, Abu Hanifa distinguishes between sitting for prayer (no liability) and other activities (liability); the two say no liability in all cases because sitting is necessary for prayer. Later authorities (Sadr al-Islam) prefer the view of the two, and the fatwa is upon that.

Key Terms
عاقلة (al-āqilah): The male agnatic relatives of a person who accidentally caused death, who bear the diyah (blood money) collectively.
تسبيب (tasbīb): Indirect causation, where a person sets up a condition that leads to harm without directly inflicting it.
مباشرة (mubāsharah): Direct act causing harm, which overrides indirect causation in liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position, is a non-member of a clan mosque liable if a lantern he hangs falls and injures someone?
A) Yes, because he is a non-member
B) No, because hanging a lantern is an act of worship
C) Yes, because he should not have done it
D) No, because the mosque is public

Key Takeaway
In a clan mosque, the mufta bihi is that no one is liable for injuries caused by hanging lanterns, spreading mats, or sitting, because these are considered acts of worship.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability for Falling Objects and the Mosque Fixtures Dispute
Ruling on liability for falling objects and intra-Hanafi dispute on mosque-related harms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between liability for carried objects and worn garments, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mosque fixtures, stating the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A carrier is liable if their load falls and causes harm; a wearer is not. For mosque fixtures, the later fatwa exempts all, following the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines the fine line between liability and exemption when an object falls and injures someone. We begin with the fundamental distinction between carrying an object and wearing a garment, then delve into a debated scenario: who is liable when someone installs a fixture or sits in a mosque, causing harm? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement, and we will see which view prevails for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling on carried objects
State that if a man carries something in the road and it falls on a person, he is liable for the injury or death (diyah). This is because carrying is mubah but conditioned on safety.
The carrier intends to preserve the object, so he must ensure its safe transport.
2
Contrast with worn garments
If the object is a garment being worn and it falls, the wearer is not liable. The key distinction is intention: the wearer intends to cover himself, not to preserve the garment, so no liability.
Imam Muhammad adds: if one wears something unusual or beyond necessity, liability may return.
3
Analyze the mosque dispute
When a person hangs a lantern, spreads a mat, or sits in a mosque and causes harm, Abu Hanifa differentiates based on identity: a member of the mosque's tribe is not liable, but an outsider is. For sitting, non-prayer acts are liable, prayer acts are not. The Sahibayn hold that no one is liable in any case because these are acts of worship (qurbah).
The dispute is over whether religious intent removes liability.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Later scholars (al-Halwani, Sadr al-Islam, al-'Ayni) adopted the Sahibayn's view, and it is the basis for fatwa. Thus, in practice, no one is liable for such mosque-related actions.
The text explicitly states 'عليه الفتوى' (upon it is the fatwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Blood Money. The carried item. The carrier owes blood money for the carried item. Whatever a man causes in the road, then falls upon a person and kills or injures him, he is liable. And if it was a garment that he was wearing and it fell, he is not liable. A mosque of a tribe: if a man among them hangs a lantern in it or places pieces of wood or a pebble and a person is killed by it, he is not liable. But if it is a mat of reeds placed in it, and a person is killed by the lantern or similar, and the person was from other than the tribe, then the person who placed it is liable. And if a man among them sits in it and someone is killed by his sitting, the sitter is liable if his action is not related to prayer; and if it is related to prayer, he is not liable. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he is not liable in both cases because this is a religious act through which one is rewarded, so it is like the mosque's own people, and as if with their permission. And there is a detailed discussion... (truncated for brevity due to length). The relied-upon position is that of the two (Sahibayn). (End of section)
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Blood Money (Diyat) with rulings on liability for objects being carried or placed in public spaces. It first establishes that if a man carries something in the road and it falls on someone, causing death or injury, he is liable. However, if he is wearing a garment and it falls, he is exempt, because the act of wearing is not inherently intended to protect the garment, whereas carrying is. Then it addresses a specific case concerning a mosque built by a particular tribe: if a member of that tribe hangs a lantern, spreads a mat, or places stones, and a person is killed, the tribal member is not liable according to Abu Hanifa, but any outsider doing the same is liable. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, saying no one is liable because these are acts of charity (qurbah) to enable the mosque's functions, and all are rewarded. The majority of later scholars, including al-Halwani, adopted the view of the Sahibayn, and this is the basis for fatwa. The passage also discusses the case of a person sitting in the mosque: if sitting for non-prayer purposes (e.g., talking), Abu Hanifa holds the sitter liable for harm caused by his sitting, but exempts sitting for prayer. The Sahibayn again exempt in all cases. The section concludes with further details about reading Qur'an, teaching, sleeping, etc., all subject to the same dispute.

Key Terms
المحمول (al-mahmul): That which is carried (an object being transported) by a person in the road.
الحامل (al-hamil): The carrier of an object in the road; he is liable if the object falls and causes harm.
الرداء (al-rida'): A garment/cloak that a person wears; if it falls and causes harm, the wearer is not liable.
عشيرة (ashirah): A tribe or specific community that built the mosque; they have special authority over its affairs.
قربة (qurbah): An act of worship or religious merit that draws one closer to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in this passage, what is the ruling on a non-tribal person who hangs a lantern in a mosque and it falls on someone, causing death?
A) He is liable for blood money (diyah).
B) He is not liable because it is an act of religious merit.
C) He is liable only if he was sitting, not if hanging.
D) He is liable only if the mosque belongs to his tribe.

Key Takeaway
Liability for harm depends on whether the object was carried (liable) or worn (not), and for mosque fixtures, the Sahibayn's view—exempting all—is the operative fatwa.

Liability for a Leaning Wall: Conditions and Istihsan
Main ruling with proof by istihsan and conditions for liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a leaning wall owner and apply the four conditions for liability.

Core Idea
A leaning wall owner is liable only if requested to demolish and fails; istihsan overrides qiyas.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq explains a nuanced ruling: though a wall collapse may seem accidental, the owner becomes liable if he ignores a demand to fix it. We will explore the istihsan proof and the four conditions that must be satisfied for liability to arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a wall leaning toward a public road that collapses and causes harm. The ruling is that the owner is liable (ḍāmin) for damages.
2
Recognize the legal method
The ruling is based on istiḥsān, not strict qiyās. By qiyās, the owner would not be liable because the collapse was not his direct act. Istihsān imposes liability because his refusal to demolish after a request makes him a transgressor.
This is a key example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Understand the justification
The istihsan argument: by refraining from demolition after a proper request while being able to do so, the owner becomes muta'addī (transgressor). Evidence from 'Alī, Shurayḥ, and other Tābi'ūn.
Liability is thus a form of ta'dī (transgression).
4
List the four conditions
Liability is conditional: (1) request from a competent person, (2) request made to the proper party, (3) requester's authority continues until collapse, (4) sufficient time passes after request.
If any condition is missing, the owner is not liable.
5
Note the practical effect
For loss of life, diya is on the 'āqila; for property, the owner is directly liable. The passage clarifies who is considered a competent requester and proper party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحائط المائل
حائط مال إلى طريق العامة ضمن ربه ما تلف به من نفس أو مال إن طالب بنقضه مسلم أو ذمي، ولم ينقضه في مدة يقدر على نقضه، أي رب الحائط بلا خلاف. وصلاة التطوع كالفرض بالإجماع. وذكر شمس الأئمة أن الصحيح من مذهب أبي حنيفة أن الجالس لانتظار الصلاة لا يضمن، وإنما الخلاف في العمل لا يكون له اختصاص بالمسجد كقراءة القرآن ودرس الفقه والحديث. وذكر في "كشف الغوامض": إن جلس لقراءة القرآن، أو معتكفا لا يضمن بالإجماع. وذكر فخر الإسلام والصدر الشهيد: أنه إن جلس للحديث ضمن بالإجماع. (عيني، تكملة) فصل إلخ: لما ذكر محله أحكام القتل الذي تعلق بالإنسان مباشرة وتسبيبا، شرع في بيان أحكام القتل الذي يتعلق بالجماد، الحائط المائل، وكان من حقها أن تؤخر عن مسائل جميع الحيوانات تقديما للحيوان على الجماد، إلا وهو أن الحائط المائل لما ناسب الجرصن والميزاب ونحوهما ألحق مسائله بها، ولذا عبر بلفظ الفصل لا بلفظ الباب. تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٧٥/٩] ضمن ربه إلخ [ أي صاحبه ولو حكما كالواقف والقيم. (طائي)] ضمن مالك في هذه الصورة وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يضمن؛ لأن البناء كان في ملكه مستقيما، والسقوط والميلان ليسا من صنعه من وجه. الاستحسان: أنه بالامتناع من النقض بعد الطلب مع القدرة عليه صار متعديا، وهو مروي عن علي وشريح والنخعي والشعبي وغيرهم من أئمة التابعين. (فتح) من نفس أو مال إلخ وما تلف به من نفس تحب الدية على العاقلة، وما تلف به من مال يجب ضمانها ملا مسكين مسلم أو ذمي رجلا كان أو امرأة حرا كان أو مكاتبا. ملا مسكين)] يعني من أهل الطلب، فخرج العبد والصبي المحجور عليهما؛ لأنهما ليسا من أهل المطالبة بحقهما، فكذا بحق العامة إلا إن أذن لهما في الخصومة. (فتح) ولم ينقضه في مدة إلخ واعلم أن لتضمين رب الحائط شروط الأول: أنه لابد من طلب مالك الحائط بنقضه؛ لأنه لا يكون متعديا إلا بالطلب. ومن الإشهاد عليه وهو أن يقول الرجل : اشهدوا أني قد تقدمت إلى هذا الرجل، يصير وطالبته بنقض هذا الحائط، لكن الإشهاد للتمكن من إثباته عند جحوده أو جحود عاقلته، فكان الاحتياط، ولهذا لم يذكره الشيخ. الثاني : أن يكون المطالبة إلى من له ولاية التفريع كالمالك والوصي في ملك الصغير أو الجد والعبد التاجر والراهن والمكاتب ولا يطالب المستأجر والمستعير والمرتهن والمودع، حتى لو سقط وأتلف شيئا لا يضمن هؤلاء. وسوّى الشيخ بين أن يكون المطالب النقض مسلما أو ذميا؛ لأن الناس كلهم شركاء في المرور، فصح الطلب من أي كان بعد أن كان بالغا عاقلا، حرا كان أو مكاتبا، ذكرا كان أو أنثى، بخلاف العبيد والصبيان المحجور عليهم. الثالث يشترط دوام تلك الولاية إلى وقت السقوط، حتى لو خرج عن ملكه بالبيع بعد الإشهاد برى عن الضمان؛ لعدم قدرته على النقض. الرابع يشترط للضمان أن تمضي مدة يتمكن فيها من النقض بعد الإشهاد حتى لو أشهد عليه، فسقط من ساعته قبل التمكن من نقضه لا يضمن ما تلف. (عيني باختصار وتصرف)
Section on the Leaning Wall. A wall that leans toward a public road: its owner is liable for whatever is destroyed by it, whether life or property, if he was requested to demolish it by a Muslim or a dhimmī, and he did not demolish it within a period in which he was able to demolish it, i.e., the owner of the wall, without dispute. And supererogatory prayer is like obligatory prayer by consensus. Shams al-A'imma mentioned that the correct position of Abū Ḥanīfa is that the one sitting waiting for prayer is not liable; rather the dispute is about an action that has no specific association with the mosque, like reciting Qur'an and studying fiqh and hadith. In Kashf al-Ghawāmiḍ it is mentioned: if he sat to recite Qur'an or as a mu'takif, he is not liable by consensus. Fakhr al-Islām and al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd mentioned: if he sat for hadith, he is liable by consensus. ('Aynī, Takmila) The section etc.: When the author mentioned the rules of killing that pertain to a human being directly or indirectly, he began to explain the rules of killing that pertain to inanimate objects, i.e., the leaning wall; and it would have been proper to delay it after all matters concerning animals, giving priority to animals over inanimate objects, except that the leaning wall, because it is akin to the projecting beam and waterspout and the like, he attached its issues to them, hence he used the term 'section' (faṣl) not 'chapter' (bāb). (Takmilat al-Baḥr al-Rā'iq 9/175) 'Its owner is liable etc.' i.e., its owner, even if by legal fiction like the waqf administrator and guardian. (Ṭā'ī) The owner is liable in this situation, and this is istiḥsān; by qiyās he would not be liable, because the building was straight on his property, and falling and leaning are not his doing in any way. The istiḥsān: because by refraining from demolition after the request while being able to do so, he becomes a transgressor; and this is narrated from 'Alī, Shurayḥ, al-Nakha'ī, al-Sha'bī and other leading tābi'ūn. (Fatḥ) 'Regarding life or property etc.': i.e., what is destroyed of life: the blood money is due on the 'āqila; what is destroyed of property: its compensation is obligatory. (Mullā Miskīn) 'Muslim or dhimmī' means one who is entitled to demand, so the slave and the minor under guardianship are excluded, because they are not entitled to demand their own rights, so similarly for the public's right, unless they are granted permission for litigation. (Fatḥ) 'And he did not demolish it within a period etc.': Know that for the owner of the wall to be liable there are conditions: First: there must be a request to the owner of the wall to demolish it, because he is not a transgressor except by request. And it is also required to call witnesses upon him, i.e., the man says: 'Bear witness that I have approached this man and demanded that he demolish this wall'; but the witnessing is to be able to prove it if he denies or his 'āqila denies; so it is a precaution, hence the Shaykh did not mention it. Second: the request must be made to one who has authority to give a ruling, like the owner, the guardian over the minor's property, the grandfather, the trading slave, the pledgor, and the mukātab. The lessee, borrower, pledgee, and depositary are not to be requested; even if it falls and destroys something, these are not liable. The Shaykh equated the one making the request whether Muslim or dhimmī, because all people are partners in passing; so the request is valid from anyone, provided he is an adult, sane, free or mukātab, male or female; unlike slaves and minors under guardianship. Third: that this authority must continue until the time of falling; so if he disposed of it by sale after witnessing, he is free from liability due to inability to demolish. Fourth: for liability, it is necessary that a period pass in which he is able to demolish after witnessing; so if he called witnesses, and it fell immediately before he was able to demolish it, he is not liable for what is destroyed. ('Aynī, abridged and modified)
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with supporting proof from the Hanafi school. It addresses the liability of the owner of a wall that leans toward a public road. The key principle: if the wall collapses and causes harm, the owner is liable (ḍāmin) only if he was requested to demolish it (ṭalab bi-naqḍihi) by any Muslim or dhimmī (non-Muslim subject), and he failed to do so within a period he was capable of demolishing it. The ruling is based on istiḥsān (juristic preference), as the text explicitly states: by qiyās (analogy) the owner would not be liable because the wall was originally built straight on his own property, and the leaning and collapse were not his direct act. However, istiḥsān holds that by refusing to demolish after being requested and being able to do so, he becomes a muta'addī (transgressor), and thus liable. This istiḥsān is attributed to early authorities: 'Alī, Shurayḥ, al-Nakha'ī, al-Sha'bī, and other Tābi'ūn. The passage then details four conditions for liability: (1) a valid request from someone entitled to demand; (2) the request made to the proper party (owner or legal representative); (3) the requester's authority must persist until the collapse; (4) a sufficient period must elapse after the request to allow demolition. The text also clarifies that the 'āqila (agnatic relatives) pays the blood money for loss of life, while property damages are the owner's direct liability. The note about supererogatory prayer (ṣalāt al-taṭawwu') seems out of place but is a parenthetical remark from the commentator. The passage type is a ruling with proof (istiḥsān), and the textual operation is an expansion (sharḥ) of the base text from Kanz al-Daqā'iq.

Key Terms
الحائط المائل (al-ḥā'iṭ al-mā'il): A wall that leans from its vertical position toward a public road or private property.
استحسان (istiḥsān): A source of law where a ruling is preferred due to considerations of equity and public interest, overriding strict analogy.
الطلب بنقضه (al-ṭalab bi-naqḍihi): A formal request made to the wall owner to demolish the leaning wall.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi ruling on leaning wall liability?
A) Qiyas
B) Istihsan
C) Ijma
D) Quran

Key Takeaway
The owner of a leaning wall becomes liable for damages if, after being requested by a competent person, he fails to demolish it within a reasonable period, based on istihsan.

Wall Built Leaning: Liability and the Role of Formal Request
Ruling on wall built leaning initially and request for leaning wall on private property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a wall owner is liable for damage from a leaning wall without a formal request, and when a request is required, including cases of shared property.

Core Idea
A wall built leaning from the start makes the owner liable without request; a wall that leans later requires a request to the affected neighbor.

In the previous lesson, we learned about walls that were built straight and later leaned, requiring the owner to make repairs. This lesson expands on two special scenarios: when a wall is built leaning from the very beginning, and when a wall leans toward private property. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for determining liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the legal act: building a wall in a leaning state (ibtidā’an) or a wall that leans after construction. The ruling: if built leaning, the owner is liable for any damage without need for a formal request (ṭalab).
This is an act of ta‘addī (transgression).
2
Differentiate initial lean from subsequent lean
If the wall was built straight and later leaned (e.g., due to soil movement), the owner is not automatically liable; a request must be made to repair. If he fails, then liability arises. But if built leaning, liability attaches immediately.
3
Apply to private property vs public road
When a wall leans toward a private house, the right of request belongs to the house owner (rabb al-dār). He can grant a delay or absolve the wall owner. For a public road, any Muslim can request, and the ruler cannot harm the public interest.
The term 'dār' is an example; applies to any private property.
4
Handle shared property cases
In a shared wall among multiple owners, if one co-owner builds without permission and damage occurs, liability is divided according to shares, with an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the exact fraction.
See the madhhab dispute map for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) فإن قيل: الواحد من الشركاء لا يقدر أن يصح تقدمه إليه؟ قلنا: إن لم يتمكن من هدم نصيبه يتمكن من إصلاحه بالمرافعة إلى الحاكم، وبه يحصل الغرض وهو إزالة الضرر. (عيني) دار بين ثلاثة: أي مشتركة بين ثلاثة أنفس. فعطب به أي بالحفر أو ببناء الحائط. And if he built it leaning from the beginning, he is liable for what is destroyed by its fall without any request. If it leans toward a man's house, then the request is to its owner; if he gives him a delay or absolves him, it is valid, unlike the public road. The wall leaning to the road in the first instance. A wall shared among five: he called witnesses against one of them, and it fell on a man; the one against whom witnesses were called is liable for one-fifth of the blood money. A house (dār) among three: one of them dug a well in it, or built a wall, and a man was killed by it; if he did so without the permission of his partner, the digger or builder is liable for two-thirds of the blood money. ('Aynī) If it is said: how can one of the partners make a valid complaint against him? We say: if he is not able to demolish his share, he is able to rectify it by taking the matter to the judge, and thereby the objective is achieved, which is removal of harm. ('Aynī) 'A house among three': i.e., jointly owned by three individuals. 'So a man was killed by it': i.e., by the digging or by building the wall. ('Aynī) 'Two-thirds of the blood money': i.e., the digger and builder are liable for two-thirds of the blood money according to Abū Ḥanīfa, and it is on his 'āqila; because he is a transgressor with respect to two shares due to ownership of his two partners; as for his own share, he is not a transgressor; and the destruction occurred by one cause, so it is divided according to shares as previously. And the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) said: upon him is half =
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents two sub-cases following the main ruling on leaning walls. First: If a wall is built leaning from the very beginning (ibtidā'), the owner is liable for any damage caused by its collapse even without any formal request (ṭalab). This is because the owner committed an act of transgression (ta'addī) by constructing a defective wall, analogous to projecting a beam or digging a pit in the public road. The text notes that the ruling does not differentiate between a slight or excessive lean; however, a reference to al-Muntaqā is given where only excessive lean triggers liability. Second: If the wall leans toward a private house (dār), the right of request belongs specifically to the owner of that house (rabb al-dār). If the house owner grants a delay (tajīl) or absolves (ibrā') the wall owner, it is valid and the wall owner is not liable if the wall falls during that period. This differs from the case of a public road, where the request is from any Muslim or dhimmī, and the ruler's permission cannot harm the public interest. The text also explains that the term 'dar' is an example, not a restriction; the same applies if an upper floor leans toward a lower floor, etc. The passage type is a ruling statement without explicit proof; it extends the earlier rule to new scenarios.

Key Terms
ابتداء (ibtidā'an): From the start; the wall was built in a leaning state.
رب الدار (rabb al-dār): Owner of the house, or anyone with authority like a lessee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only after a formal request from the affected party.
B) Without any need for a formal request.
C) Only if the lean is excessive.
D) Only if the wall is on shared property.

Key Takeaway
A wall built leaning from the start imposes immediate liability on the owner, while a wall that later leans requires the neighbor's request first.

Apportioning Diya in Shared Property Torts: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on liability division in shared wall and courtyard cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's proportional liability rule in shared property torts and distinguish it from the Sahibayn's equal-division approach.

Core Idea
Liability for harm on shared property is apportioned by ownership shares, not equally, because transgression only exists regarding others' shares.

In this passage, we encounter a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The question: when a co-owner causes death through an act on common property without permission, is liability divided according to the value of the shares wrongfully used, or equally? The answer reveals a fundamental difference in how each imam conceptualizes the transgression (ta'addi) and its relationship to ownership. We'll walk through both cases and trace the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two fact patterns
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) a wall shared among five co-owners, where one co-owner is formally requested to demolish his portion but the wall collapses and kills a man; (2) a house shared among three co-owners, where one co-owner digs a well or builds a wall without permission, causing a death.
Both cases involve a co-owner committing an act that leads to death on common property without the consent of all partners.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for each
For the wall case, Abu Hanifa rules the warned co-owner pays 1/5 of the diya (because only his share was the subject of the warning). For the house case, the digger/builder pays 2/3 of the diya (because he wrongfully used the shares of the other two co-owners, i.e., 2/3 of the property).
Abu Hanifa's key principle: liability is proportional to the shares in which the act was a transgression.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling for each
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that in both cases the liable co-owner pays 1/2 of the diya. They consider the death as a single cause, and thus liability should be divided equally between the actor's own share and the combined shares of the others.
The text only gives their position for the house case ('upon him is half'), but it is inferred they apply the same half-rule to the wall case.
4
Analyze the 'illa (cause) behind each ruling
For Abu Hanifa, the 'illa is the transgression: the act is wrongful only with respect to the shares of other co-owners; there is no transgression against one's own share. Hence liability is divided by ownership shares of the offended parties. For the Sahibayn, the 'illa is the single act of causation: one act led to the death, so liability should be split equally between the part owned by the actor and the part owned by others.
This difference in 'illa leads directly to the different numerical outcomes.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a 5-share wall case, Abu Hanifa says 1/5 diya; Sahibayn say 1/2 diya. In a 3-share house case, Abu Hanifa says 2/3 diya; Sahibayn say 1/2 diya. The difference can be significant depending on the ownership proportions.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's proportional view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents two intra-Hanafi disputes between Imam Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad al-Shaybānī) regarding liability in shared property. The first case: a wall jointly owned by five people. One of them is formally requested (through witnesses) to demolish his portion, but the wall collapses and kills a man. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, that specific co-owner is liable for one-fifth of the blood money (diya), because the request and transgression pertain only to his share. The ṣāḥibayn hold that he is liable for half (nisf) – but the text for this first case does not explicitly state their position here; however, from the second case, we see their reasoning. The second case: a house (dār) jointly owned by three. One partner digs a well or builds a wall without permission, causing a death. Abū Ḥanīfa holds that the digger/builder is liable for two-thirds of the diya, because he is a transgressor (muta'addī) with respect to the shares of his two partners (i.e., 2/3 of the property), while as for his own share, he is not a transgressor. Since the death is caused by a single act, the liability is apportioned according to ownership shares. The ṣāḥibayn, however, hold that he is liable for half. Their reasoning (inferred from similar texts) is that the death is caused by a single cause, so it should be divided equally between the share that is his own and the combined share of the others. Alternatively, they may consider the entire act as one transgression leading to equal responsibility. The passage also addresses an objection: how can a co-owner legally act against the offending partner? The answer: if he cannot demolish his share alone, he can resort to the judge to compel removal of harm. This dispute illustrates the Hanafi methodology of using 'illa (effective cause) and qiyas to derive rulings.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): Jointly owned property, e.g., a wall or house shared by multiple owners.
الحصة (al-ḥiṣṣa): Share; the portion of property owned by each co-owner.
متعد (muta'addī): Transgressor; one who exceeds proper bounds and causes harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the house shared among three, what is the Sahibayn's ruling for the diya of the digger?
A) One-third diya
B) Half diya
C) Two-thirds diya
D) Full diya

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for harm on joint property is divided according to ownership shares where the transgression occurred, not equally, following Abu Hanifa's view.

Diyat — Blood Money

8 min
Liability for a Shared Wall: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Diyah Amount
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for shared wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the blood money owed by a co-owner of a shared wall who was requested to demolish, and explain the rationale behind each position.

Core Idea
The blood money is divided according to ownership shares (Abu Hanifa) or according to attribution of the cause (Sahibayn).

When a shared wall falls and kills someone, who pays the blood money? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a scenario where one co-owner is requested to demolish the wall but cannot. Abu Hanifa holds that the requested owner pays only his share of the blood money, while the Sahibayn hold he pays half. Understanding their differing reasoning sharpens your ability to apply the principles of causation and liability.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Five co-owners share a wall. One owner is requested by a passerby to demolish it, but he cannot demolish his share alone. The wall falls and kills the passerby.
2
Identify the dispute
Abu Hanifa says the requested owner pays 1/5 (his share) of the blood money. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say he pays 1/2.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Death occurred from a single cause (the fall). Therefore, the blood money is attributed to the single cause and then divided among all owners according to their ownership shares.
The cause is one, so liability is proportional to ownership.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The destruction is only considered with respect to the share of the requested owner; the shares of the other owners (not requested) are wasted. Thus, liability is divided in half between the requested owner's share and the wasted portion.
Attribution is to the requested owner's share only.
5
Practical outcome and objection
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's 1/5. An objection that the requested owner cannot demolish his share alone is answered: he can take the matter to the judge to have the entire wall demolished to remove harm.
The judge can order full demolition, preserving the owner's right to remedy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خمس الدية أي ضمن الذي أشهد عليه الطالب خمس الدية وتكون على عاقلته عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا عليه نصف الدية؛ لأن التلف بنصيب من أشهد عليه معتبر وبنصيب من لم يشهد عليه هدر، فانقسم على نصفين. [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/٢] وللإمام أن الموت حصل بعلة واحدة، وهي القتل فيضاف التلف إلى العلة الواحدة، ثم يقسم على أربابها بقدر الملك تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۷۹/۹] فإن قيل: الواحد من الشركاء لا يقدر أن يصح تقدمه إليه؟ قلنا: إن لم يتمكن من هدم نصيبه يتمكن من إصلاحه بالمرافعة إلى الحاكم، وبه يحصل الغرض وهو إزالة الضرر. (عيني)
One-fifth of the blood money: i.e., the one who is testified against (the requested one) is liable for one-fifth of the blood money, and it is upon his ʿāqila according to Abu Hanifa. And they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: upon him is half the blood money, because the destruction is considered with respect to the share of the one testified against, and with respect to the share of the one not testified against it is wasted, so it is divided into halves. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa): death occurred from one cause, which is killing, so the destruction is attributed to the single cause, then divided among its owners according to ownership. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Rā’iq) If it is said: the one individual among the partners cannot validly proceed to him? We say: if he is unable to demolish his share, he is able to remedy it by bringing the matter to the judge, and thereby the purpose is achieved, which is removal of harm. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a shared wall among five owners. One owner is requested by a passerby to demolish the wall, but he cannot demolish his share alone. If the wall falls and kills someone, Abu Hanifa holds that the requested owner pays one-fifth of the blood money (his share), and his ʿāqila (tribal guarantors) bear it. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Ṣāḥibayn) say he pays half, because the damage is attributed proportionally: the share of the requested owner is considered, while the other owners' shares are irrelevant (since they were not asked), so the liability is divided in half. Abu Hanifa argues that death is caused by a single act (the fall), so the total blood money is divided among all owners according to their ownership shares. An objection that the requested owner cannot demolish only his share is answered: he can go to the judge to have the entire wall demolished to remove the harm.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The paternal relatives who collectively pay blood money (diyah) on behalf of a person who causes unintentional killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The full blood money.
B) Half of the blood money.
C) One-fifth of the blood money.
D) Nothing, because he was unable to demolish.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute over liability from a shared wall, the relied-upon position is that the requested owner pays blood money proportional to his ownership share.

Diyat — Blood Money

Digging Well or Building in Shared Property without Permission
Determines liability when one partner digs a well or builds without permission in shared land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the blood money liability when a partner causes death by an act on shared property, and explain the principle of dividing liability by share of ownership.

Core Idea
Liability for death from an unpermitted act in shared property is divided by the shares where the actor is a transgressor.

When a partner in a shared property digs a well or builds a structure without the consent of the other partners, and someone dies as a result, who is liable for the blood money? The Hanafi school addresses this by considering the actor's status as a transgressor in the shares of the other partners, but not in his own. This leads to an intra-madhhab disagreement on the precise fraction: Abu Hanifa says two-thirds, while the Sahibayn say half. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal act
Three persons share a house. One of them, without permission, digs a well or builds a wall. This act causes the death of someone. The legal question: how much blood money (diyah) is the actor liable for?
The act is considered transgressive because it infringes on the rights of the other partners.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that the actor is a transgressor in the two shares belonging to his partners (2/3 of the property) but not in his own share (1/3). Since the death resulted from a single cause, liability is divided by the shares in which he was a transgressor. Therefore, he pays 2/3 of the blood money.
The principle is 'division by share of ownership' where transgression occurs.
3
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)
The Sahibayn argue that the act can be divided into two halves: non-transgressive for his own share and transgressive for the others'. Thus, liability is split equally, and he pays 1/2 of the blood money.
They view the act as having two aspects, rather than being a single undivided cause.
4
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Both agree on transgression in the partners' shares, but differ on how to apportion the liability. Abu Hanifa treats the act as a single cause divided by ownership shares, while the Sahibayn treat the act as having two separable components.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Note the Shafi'i position and practical consequence
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that for digging (but not building) the actor is liable for the full blood money, because digging is inherently dangerous. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a mufti may follow either Abu Hanifa's 2/3 or the Sahibayn's 1/2 depending on the preferred view.
The Shafi'i view is outside the Hanafi school, but mentioned for contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دار بين ثلاثة: أي مشتركة بين ثلاثة أنفس. فعطب به أي بالحفر أو ببناء الحائط. (عيني) ثلثي الدية: أي ضمن الحافر والباني ثلثي الدية عند أبي حنيفة، وتكون على عاقلته؛ لأنه متعد في الحصتين باعتبار ملك شريكيه وأما في ملكه فهو ليس بمتعد، وقد حصل التلف بعلة واحدة، فتقسم بالحصة كما مر، وقالا: عليه نصف الدية كما في المسألة الأولى؛ لأنه في الحفر والبناء باعتبار ملكه غير متعد وباعتبار ملك شريكيه متعد، فكانا قسمين، فانقسم عليهما نصفين وعند الشافعي: يضمن كل الدية في الحفر فقط. (فتح عيني)
A house among three: i.e., shared among three persons. So he was killed by it, i.e., by the digging or by building the wall. (Ayni) Two-thirds of the blood money: i.e., the digger and the builder are liable for two-thirds of the blood money according to Abu Hanifa, and it is upon his ʿāqila, because he transgresses in the two shares with regard to his two partners' ownership, but in his own share he is not a transgressor. And the destruction occurred from a single cause, so it is divided by share as has passed. And they two (Sahibayn) said: upon him is half the blood money, as in the first issue, because in digging and building, with regard to his own property he is not a transgressor, and with regard to his partners' property he is a transgressor, so there are two divisions, so it is divided into halves. And according to al-Shafi'i: he is liable for the full blood money in digging only. (Fath and Ayni)
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with mention of the Shafi'i position. Three persons share a house; one of them digs a well or builds a structure without the others' permission. If someone is killed by this, Abu Hanifa says the digger/builder pays 2/3 of the blood money (his own share is excluded because he is not a transgressor regarding his own portion, but he is a transgressor regarding the two-thirds belonging to his partners). The Sahibayn say he pays half, because the act is neutral regarding his own share and transgressive regarding the others', so liability is divided equally. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that he pays the full blood money for digging (but not for building, presumably because digging is more dangerous).

Key Terms
متعد (muta‘addī): One who acts with transgression (ta‘addī) by infringing on others' rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how much blood money does a partner pay if he digs a well without permission in shared property and someone dies?
A) Full blood money.
B) Half blood money.
C) Two-thirds blood money.
D) Nothing, because he owned part of the property.

Key Takeaway
In shared property, an unpermitted act causing death results in blood money liability proportional to the shares where the actor is a transgressor, with Abu Hanifa's view of 2/3 being the relied-upon position.

Rider's Liability for Animal's Actions
Explains basic rule and exceptions for rider's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for rider liability, identify which animal actions trigger liability, and apply the exception for kicks.

Core Idea
A rider is not liable for an animal's kick unless he stops it — because what cannot be avoided cannot be punished.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule on a rider's liability for harm caused by his animal. The key principle is that liability attaches for preventable actions but not for unavoidable ones, particularly kicks. Understanding this rule is essential for cases involving animal-related accidents and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify liable actions
List the animal actions for which the rider is liable: trampling with hand, foot, head; biting with front teeth; striking with hand.
These actions are considered preventable because they are not a necessary part of riding.
2
Identify the exempt action
Note that the rider is not liable for a kick with the foot or tail, as this cannot be easily avoided while riding.
The hadith 'al-rijlu jubbar' confirms the exemption.
3
Identify the exception to the exemption
If the rider stops the animal in the road, he becomes liable for kicks because stopping is a deliberate act that could have been avoided.
Stopping in places like mosque courtyards or markets is also considered, but with additional rules.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold the rider liable for kicks even without stopping, relying on general liability principles. The Hanafi response is that the hadith and impossibility of avoidance override general principles.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمن الراكب ما أوطات دابته بيد أو رجل أو رأس أو كدمت أو خبطت، لا ما نفحت برجل أو ذنب إلا إذا أوقفها في الطريق، فإن أصابت بيدها أو رجلها . أي الدابة الدابة لا يضمن الدية كما في المسألة الأولى؛ لأنه في الحفر والبناء باعتبار ملكه غير متعد وباعتبار ملك شريكيه متعد، فكانا قسمين، فانقسم عليهما نصفين وعند الشافعي: يضمن كل الدية في الحفر فقط. (فتح عيني) باب جناية إلخ : قال في "التكملة : لما فرغ به من بيان أحكام جناية الإنسان شرع في بيان جناية البهيمة، ولا شك في تقدم جناية الإنسان على البهيمة كذا في "النهاية"، ويرد عليه أنه لم يفرغ من بيان جناية الإنسان مطلقا بل بقي منها جناية المملوك، ولا شك أنه من الإنسان فيقدم على البهيمة، وكان من حقه أن يقدم على جناية البهيمة كذا في "غاية البيان". [تكملة البحر الرائق: ۱۸۱/۹] وقال في "الفتح": عن أحمد بن يونس كان من حق هذا الباب أن يذكر بعد جناية المملوك لفضيلة النطق في المملوك، لكن لما كانت البهيمة ملحقة بالجماد من حيث عدم العقل، ألحق هذا الباب بباب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق. البهيمة البهيمة كل ذات أربع قوائم ولو في البحر، والجمع بهائم. (فتح) ضمن الراكب إلخ: اعلم أن الأصل في مسائل هذا الباب: أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح بشرط السلامة فيما يمكن الاحتراز عنه؛ لأنه تصرف في حقه من وجه، وفي حق غيره من وجه؛ لكونه مشتركا بين كل الناس فقلنا بالإجازة مقيدا بشرط السلامة ليعتدل النظر الجانبين فيما يمكن الاحتراز لا فيما لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى منع التصرف، والاحتراز عن الإيطاء والكدم والصدم والخبط ممكن؛ لأنه ليس من ضرورة السير، وقيدناه بشرط السلامة والكدم: العض بمقدم الأسنان، والخبط : الضرب باليد تكملة عيني رد المحتار لا ما نفحت إلخ أي لا يضمن الراكب ما نفحت دابته برجل أو ذنب؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الاحتراز عن النفحة بالرجل والذنب مع السير على الدابة فلم نقيده بالسلامة، كما الأصل يقال: نفحه برجله إذا ضربه وهو بالحاء المهملة من باب فعل يفعل بالفتح فيهما. رمز الحقائق: ٤٥٢/٢] إلا إذا أوقفها إلخ استثناء من قوله : "لا" ما نفحت" يعني لا يضمن في النفحة بالرجل والذنب إلا إذا أوقف الدابة في الطريق، فنفحت برجلها أو ذنبها فحينئذ يضمن؛ لأنه يمكنه الاحتراز عن الإيقاف، وإن لم يمكنه . فصار متعديا بالإيقاف فيضمنه، وعند الشافعي وأحمد في رواية: يضمن ما نفحت من غير توقيفه أيضا، وعند لا ضمان في فعل الرجل والذنب أوقف أو لم يوقف وباب المسجد كالطريق في الإيقاف، ولو جعل الإمام موضعا لوقوف الدواب عند باب المسجد، فلا ضمان فيما حدث من الوقوف، وكذا إيقاف الدابة في سوق الدواب؛ لأنه مأذون له من جهة السلطان وكذلك الفلاة وطريق مكة إذا أوقف في غير المحجة؛ لأنه لا يضر بالناس، فلا يحتاج فيه إلى الإذن، أما المحجة فهي كالطريق
The rider is liable for what his animal tramples with its hand, foot, or head, or bites, or strikes, except for what it kicks with its foot or tail unless he stops it in the road. If it hits with its hand or foot... meaning the animal, the animal is not liable for the blood-money as in the first case; because in digging and building, with regard to his ownership he is not transgressing, and with regard to the ownership of his two partners he is transgressing, so they become two categories, and they are divided between them in halves. According to al-Shafi'i: he is liable for all the blood-money in digging only. (Fath 'Ayni) Chapter of injury etc.: He said in al-Takmilah: When he finished explaining the rulings of injury by humans, he began explaining injury by animals, and there is no doubt that injury by humans takes precedence over injury by animals, as in al-Nihayah. It is objected that he did not finish explaining injury by humans absolutely, rather there remains injury by slaves, and there is no doubt that it is from humans and takes precedence over injury by animals, and it was his right to preface it before injury by animals, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 181/9] And he said in al-Fath: From Ahmad ibn Yunus, it was right for this chapter to be mentioned after injury by slaves due to the virtue of speech in the slave, but since the animal is attached to inanimate objects in terms of lack of intellect, he attached this chapter to the chapter on what a man causes in the road. The animal: every quadruped, even in the sea, plural is baha'im. (Fath) 'The rider is liable etc.': Know that the principle in the issues of this chapter is that passing in the road of the Muslims is permissible on condition of safety in what can be avoided; because it is a disposition in his right in one way, and in the right of others in another way, since it is shared among all people. So we ruled with permission restricted by the condition of safety to balance the two sides regarding what can be avoided, not regarding what cannot be avoided, because it would lead to preventing disposition. Avoidance of trampling, biting, hitting, and striking is possible because it is not a necessity of travel, and we restricted it with the condition of safety. Al-kadm: biting with the front teeth. Al-khabt: striking with the hand. (Takmalah, 'Ayni, Radd al-Muhtar) 'Except what it kicks etc.': i.e., the rider is not liable for what his animal kicks with its foot or tail, because he cannot avoid the kick with the foot and tail while riding the animal, so we did not restrict it with safety, as the principle: one says 'he kicked him with his foot' when he strikes him, and it is with the unpointed ha', from the form fa'ala yaf'ulu with fatha on both. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 452/2) 'Unless he stops it etc.': an exception from his saying 'except what it kicks', meaning he is not liable for the kick with the foot and tail unless he stops the animal in the road, and it kicks with its foot or tail, then he is liable, because he can avoid stopping, even if he cannot avoid the kick. So he becomes a transgressor by stopping, so he is liable. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration: he is liable for what it kicks without his stopping as well. According to us: there is no liability for the action of the foot and tail, whether he stops or does not stop. The mosque courtyard is like the road regarding stopping. If the Imam makes a place for stopping animals at the mosque door, then there is no liability for what occurs from stopping. Likewise stopping an animal in the animal market, because he is permitted by the authority. Likewise the desert and the road to Makkah, if he stops off the beaten track, because he does not harm people, so no permission is needed. As for the beaten track, it is like the road.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on injuries caused by animals and injuries to them. The author establishes the general rule: the rider (raakib) of an animal is liable for harm caused by the animal's actions with its hands, feet, head, biting, or striking, because these are preventable. However, he is not liable for harm from a kick with the leg or tail, as this cannot be easily avoided while riding. An exception exists if the rider stops the animal in the road, then he is liable for kicks because stopping is a deliberate act that could have been avoided. The passage also discusses differing rulings: al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold the rider liable even without stopping, while the Hanafi school maintains no liability. Additionally, special locations like mosque courtyards, animal markets, and desert paths have their own rules. The underlying principle is that passing through public roads is permissible only with the condition of safety where avoidance is possible. The term 'kadm' is defined as biting with front teeth, and 'khabt' as striking with the hand.

Key Terms
ضمّن (dammanna): To hold liable for compensation (daman).
نفحت (nafahat): Kicked with foot or tail.
كدمت (kadamat): Bit with front teeth.
خبطت (khabatat): Struck with hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, under what condition is a rider liable for injury caused by his animal's kick?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances
B) Only if he stops the animal in the road
C) Only if the animal kicks with its leg
D) Never, because kicks are always unavoidable

Key Takeaway
A rider is not liable for an animal's kick unless he stops it.

Liability for Animal-Caused Harm: Stones, Dust, Excrement, and Urine
Details liability for various animal-caused injuries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between scenarios where a rider is liable and not liable for harm caused by his animal, based on the nature of the object and the rider's control.

Core Idea
A rider is liable only if the harm was preventable; unavoidable harm from small objects or natural bodily functions incurs no liability.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines specific cases of liability for harm caused by an animal's actions. The key principle is the rider's ability to prevent the harm: small objects stirred up by movement are unavoidable, so no liability; but large objects or actions beyond normal movement (like digging a ditch) carry liability. Similarly, excrement and urine are natural and unavoidable during movement, but if the rider unnecessarily stops the animal, liability arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects and actions
List all items mentioned in the text: pebble (حصاة), date stone (نواة), dust (غبار), small stone (حجر صغير), large stone (حجر كبير), causing a ditch (أغارها), ruining a garment (أفسد ثوبا), excrement (راثت), urine (بالت).
Each item has a specific ruling.
2
Classify each as unavoidable or avoidable
Small items (pebble, date stone, dust, small stone) are unavoidably stirred up by animal movement. Excrement/urine during movement or when stopped for that purpose are natural and unavoidable. Large items (large stone, ditch, ruined garment) are avoidable because they are not inevitable. Excrement/urine when stopped for another reason are also avoidable.
The key factor is whether the rider could have prevented the harm.
3
Determine liability for each
No liability for unavoidable: small items, and excrement/urine when moving or stopped for that purpose. Liability for avoidable: large stone, ditch, ruined garment, and excrement/urine when stopped for an unrelated reason.
Liability (daman) is wajib when harm is preventable.
4
Analyze the exception for stopping
The text explicitly distinguishes between stopping for the purpose of excreting/urinating (no liability) and stopping for any other reason (liability). This shows that the rider's intention and action matter.
The word 'أوقفها لذلك' vs 'أوقفها لغير الروث أو البول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصاة أو نواة، أو أثارت غبارا أو حجرا صغيرا ففقأ عينا لم يضمن، ولو كبيرا الدابة أو أغارها أو أفسد ثوبا الراكب ضمن، فإن راثت أو بالت في الطريق لم يضمن من عطب به وإن أوقفها لذلك، وإن أوقفها لغير الروث أو البول، فراثت أو بالت فعطب به إنسان ضمن
If it raises a pebble or a date stone, or stirs up dust or a small stone and knocks out an eye, [the rider] is not liable. But if a large stone, or if it causes a ditch or ruins a garment, the rider is liable. If the animal excretes or urinates in the road, [the rider] is not liable for whoever perishes due to it, even if he stopped it for that purpose. But if he stopped it for other than excrement or urine, and it excreted or urinated and a person perished thereby, he is liable.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage deals with specific scenarios of animal-caused harm. The baseline rule is that harm from small objects like pebbles, date stones, dust, or small stones (which are inevitably part of the animal's movement) does not incur liability, as avoidance is impossible. However, if the animal kicks up a large stone, or causes a ditch (by digging), or ruins a garment, the rider is liable because these are preventable. For excrement and urine, the rider is not liable if the animal is moving or even if stopped specifically for that natural need, as this is inherent and unavoidable. But if the rider stops the animal for some other reason, and then the animal excretes or urinates causing harm, the rider is liable because the stopping was unnecessary and constituted transgression.

Key Terms
حصاة (hasah): A small pebble or stone.
نواة (nawah): A date stone or pit.
أغارها (aghārahā): Caused a ditch or hole.
عطب (aṭiba): Perished, was harmed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a rider liable for harm caused by his animal's excrement or urine?
A) Always, because it is his property
B) Only if the animal is moving
C) Only if he stopped the animal for a reason other than excreting/urinating
D) Never, because it is natural

Key Takeaway
Liability for animal-caused harm depends on whether the rider could have prevented it; unavoidable small items and natural bodily functions during necessary movement incur no liability.

Liability and Expiation for Animal-Caused Death: Driver, Leader, and Rider
Extends liability to driver/leader and adds expiation for rider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct causation (mubasharah) and indirect causation (tasabbub) in Hanafi diyat law, apply the rule that kaffarah is only for the direct killer, and identify who is liable in scenarios involving a rider, driver, leader, and radif.

Core Idea
Kaffarah attaches to the direct killer (rider) not the indirect cause (driver/leader) — weight of the rider makes him a direct agent in trampling deaths.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq expands the liability rules for animal-caused injuries, distinguishing civil damages from religious expiation. While the driver and leader share civil liability (daman) with the rider, the expiation (kaffarah) is uniquely imposed on the rider because his weight adds to the fatal impact. The text uses a powerful analogy to a sleeping person who rolls onto another — clarifying the Hanafi principle that kaffarah requires direct physical causation, not mere indirect motive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal consequences
The passage discusses two legal outcomes: civil liability (daman) and religious expiation (kaffarah). Note that the rule 'whatever the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are also liable' refers to daman only.
Daman includes blood money (diya) and compensation; kaffarah is a separate religious obligation.
2
Understand the special case of trampling (i’tā’)
Kaffarah applies only when the animal tramples (i’tā’) a person. In other types of harm, no kaffarah is due on any of the parties.
The text specifies 'This is specifically in the case of trampling.'
3
Distinguish the roles: rider, driver, leader, radif
The rider is a direct cause (mubashir) due to his weight increasing the impact. The driver (sa'iq) and leader (qa'id) are indirect causes (mutasabbib). The radif (one seated behind) is like the rider in all rulings.
Direct causation triggers kaffarah; indirect causation does not, even though civil liability is shared.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to a sleeping person
The rider is likened to a sleeping person who rolls onto another and kills him. Both are direct killers because their body weight contributes to the death. The analogy establishes that the rider's weight makes him a direct cause, so kaffarah is wajib on him.
This analogy distinguishes the rider from the driver/leader, who do not add weight to the fatal action.
5
Apply the ruling to inheritance and bequest
The same distinction applies to deprivation of inheritance and bequest: the rider loses these rights, but the driver and leader do not, because these penalties follow direct causation in killing.
This shows the consistent application of the principle across multiple areas of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ضمنه الراكب ضمنه السائق والقائد، وعلى الراكب الكفارة لا على السائق والقائد، وهذا في الإيطاء خاصة، وأما في غير الإيطاء فلا كفارة على أحد منهم، وحاصله : أنه لو وطئت الدابة إنسانا وهو راكبها يلزمه الكفارة؛ لأنه مباشر للقتل حقيقة لثقله كالنائم إذا انقلب على إنسان فقتله، ولو وطئت الدابة وهو يسوقها أو يقودها لا يلزم السائق أو القائد الكفارة؛ لأنهما متسببان بمعنى أنه لو لا السوق أو القود لم يوجد الوطء، والكفارة جزاء المباشرة، وكذلك يتعلق بالإيطاء في حق الراكب حرمان الميراث والوصية دون السائق والقائد ومثل الراكب في هذا الحكم الرديف
Whatever the rider is liable for, the driver and leader are also liable. And the expiation is on the rider, not on the driver or leader, and this is specifically in the case of trampling. As for other than trampling, there is no expiation on any of them. The summary is: if the animal tramples a person while he is riding it, the rider is obligated to expiation because he is directly the killer in reality due to his weight, like a sleeping person who rolls onto a person and kills him. But if the animal tramples while he is driving or leading, the driver or leader is not obligated to expiation because they are indirect causes (mutasabbib) in the sense that without driving or leading, the trampling would not occur, and expiation is the recompense for direct causation. Similarly, the deprivation of inheritance and bequest attaches to the rider regarding trampling, not to the driver or leader. And the one seated behind (radif) is like the rider in this ruling.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This lesson extends the legal consequences of animal-caused harm to other persons involved with the animal. The rule is that whatever civil liability (daman) attaches to the rider also attaches to the driver (saa'iq) and leader (qaa'id), because they are direct causes too. However, there is a nuanced difference regarding expiation (kaffarah): if the animal tramples and kills a person, only the rider must perform kaffarah (freeing a slave or fasting), because he is considered a direct killer due to his weight adding to the impact, analogous to a sleeping person who rolls onto someone. The driver and leader are only indirect causes (tasabbub), so no kaffarah on them. The same distinction applies to deprivation of inheritance and bequest: the rider loses the right to inherit from or receive a bequest from the victim, but not the driver/leader. The one riding behind (radif) is treated like the rider. This highlights the Hanafi principle of distinguishing direct causation from indirect causation in liability.

Key Terms
سائق (sā'iq): The person driving the animal from behind.
قائد (qā'id): The person leading the animal by a rope.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for accidental killing: freeing a slave or fasting two months.
رديف (radīf): The person seated behind the rider on the same animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who must pay kaffarah if an animal tramples and kills a person while being ridden?
A) The rider only
B) The driver only
C) The leader only
D) Both rider and driver

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, expiation (kaffarah) for accidental killing by an animal is only due from the rider, not the driver or leader, because the rider's weight makes him a direct cause.

Liability for Stones Kicked by an Animal
Ruling on liability for animal kicking stones

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between liability and no liability for injuries caused by an animal kicking stones, based on the size of the stone and the possibility of avoidance.

Core Idea
Avoidable harm incurs liability; unavoidable harm does not.

The Hanafi madhhab provides a nuanced ruling for injuries caused by animals. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario: an animal kicks up a stone that injures someone. The key factor is not simply that the animal caused the injury, but whether the rider could have prevented it. Let's analyze the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (1) small stone / dust / date-pit → no liability; (2) large stone → liability. Write down the ruling for each.
The text explicitly contrasts 'saghir' (small) with 'kabir' (large).
2
Locate the effective cause (ʿilla)
The commentary explains that guarding against small stones is impossible (mutʿazzir) because the animal is never free of them. For large stones, guarding is possible. Identify the ʿilla as 'possibility of avoidance' (imkān al-taḥarruz).
The ruling is based on istihsan, not strict analogy.
3
Understand the underlying istihsan
Why does the Hanafi school prefer this distinction? Because imposing liability for unavoidable accidents would cause hardship (ḥaraj) and contradict the principle of ease. Istihsan allows a departure from strict qiyas in favor of practical necessity.
This is a classic example of istihsan in action.
4
Apply the ʿilla to other cases
Consider if the animal kicks mud, water, or a piece of wood. Would the same principle apply? Think about whether the object is ordinarily avoidable. Extend the reasoning to modern equivalents like a car kicking up gravel.
The key is the rider's ability to control the animal to prevent the incident.
5
Contrast with strict liability
Some might assume that any injury caused by an animal always makes the owner liable. The Hanafi school rejects this, showing that liability depends on culpability (tafrit). Recognize that this ruling protects riders from liability for normal, unavoidable incidents.
The burden of proof is on the claimant to show that the stone was large and avoidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات
جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها
حصاة أو نواة، أو أثارت غبارا أو حجرا صغيرا ففقأ عينا لم يضمن، ولو كبيرا ضمن
أو نواة: واحدة النوى وهو حب التمر وغيره. لم يضمن أي لم يضمن فيما إذا أصابت بيدها أو رجلها حصاة أو نواة أو أثارت غبارا أو حجرا صغيرا، ففقأت عينا؛ لأن التحرز عن الحجارة الصغار والغبار متعذر؛ لأن الدابة لا يخلو عنه، وإذا أثارت حجرا كبيرا ضمن؛ لأن التحرز عن الحجارة الكبار ممكن، وإنما يكون إثارة الحجار الكبار من : قلة هداية الراكب فيضمن تكملة البحر الرائق: ١٨٢/٩] ولو كبيرا ولو أثارت حجرا كبيرا.
Book of Blood-Money. Chapter on Injury caused by an animal and injury upon it. [If the animal struck with its hand or foot] a pebble or a date-pit, or stirred up dust or a small stone and knocked out an eye, there is no liability; if [it stirred up] a large stone, there is liability. Commentary: 'nawa' is singular of 'nawa' which is the pit of dates and other fruits. 'He is not liable' means he is not liable when it strikes with its hand or foot a pebble or a date-pit, or stirs up dust or a small stone and knocks out an eye, because guarding against small stones and dust is impossible, since the animal is never free from them. If it stirs up a large stone, he is liable because guarding against large stones is possible, and stirring up large stones only occurs due to the rider's lack of guidance (control), so he is liable.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on injuries caused by animals. The text states that if an animal, by its own movement, kicks up a small pebble or dust that causes injury, the rider is not liable because such small objects are unavoidable; the animal cannot move without them. However, if it kicks up a large stone, the rider is liable because he could have controlled the animal to avoid this, and its occurrence is a sign of negligence (qillat hidayat al-rakib). The commentary emphasizes the 'illa (effective cause) of possibility of avoidance. This is a Hanafi ruling based on istihsan and practical necessity, distinguishing between what is ordinary and what is excessive.

Key Terms
تحرز (taḥarruz): Avoidance or precaution; the ability to guard against harm.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for harm caused.
جناية (jināyah): An injurious act, typically resulting in bodily harm or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage
