Who Bears Qasamah for a Body Found in a House?
Ruling on qasamah with intra-Hanafi dispute and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when a dead body is found in a house, explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and identify the condition that triggers the owner's liability.

Core Idea
Owner liable only if claim is against him; resident liable per Abu Yusuf.

This passage addresses a scenario that combines property law, liability, and criminal investigation: when a dead body is discovered inside someone's house, who bears the responsibility? The Hanafi school applies the principle of yad (possession) to determine liability, but there is a significant disagreement within the school about whether the owner or the resident is responsible. Furthermore, the liability is not automatic; it depends on the direction of the murder claim.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling
The matn says the owner is liable for qasamah even if the house is empty. This is the default ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The ruling applies to the owner's 'aqilah, who pay the diyah.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf differs: if there is a resident (tenant), the resident is more specific in possession (akhas bi al-yad), so the resident's 'aqilah bears the qasamah. If the house is vacant, the owner is liable.
This difference is based on the concept of yad.
3
Analyze the condition for liability
From Tatarkhaniyya: the owner's liability is contingent on the guardian of the deceased claiming murder against the owner. If the claim is against someone else, the owner is not liable.
This condition prevents automatic liability.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., body found in empty house vs. rented house, claim against owner vs. third party), determine who is liable.
Use the majority view and note the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وجد في دار إنسان فعليه القسامة إلخ يعني على صاحب الدار القسامة ولو كانت الدار خالية وفارغة، قال في "مختصر": في دار فارغة مغلقة وجد فيها قتيل تجب القسامة والدية على عاقلة الدار عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله، وجعل أبو يوسف الوجوب على الساكن; لأنه أخص باليد، فإذا لم يكن فصاحب الدار أخص باليد، ثم وجوب الدية على عاقلة رب الدار مقيد بما إذا ادعى القتل عليه، ولهذا نقل شيخنا عن "التتارخانية" ما نصه: ادعى ولي القتيل القتل على صاحب الدار تجب الدية والقسامة على صاحب الدار، وإن ادعى القتل على رجل آخر، فلا تجب القسامة والدية على صاحب الدار. (فتح)
And if a slain person is found in a house of a man, then upon him is the qasamah etc., meaning upon the owner of the house is the qasamah even if the house is empty and vacant. He said in 'Mukhtasar': In a vacant locked house in which a slain person is found, the qasamah and blood-money are obligatory upon the 'aqilah of the house according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, and Abu Yusuf made the obligation upon the resident because he is more specific in possession. If there is no resident, then the owner is more specific in possession. Then the obligation of blood-money upon the 'aqilah of the owner is conditioned upon the claim of murder against him, for this reason our teacher narrated from 'Tatarkhaniyya' its text: If the guardian of the slain claims murder against the owner of the house, then the blood-money and qasamah are obligatory upon the owner of the house; and if he claims murder against another man, then the qasamah and blood-money are not obligatory upon the owner of the house.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a dead body is found inside someone's house. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad on one side, and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other. The general rule is that the owner of the house is liable for the qasamah and diyah, even if the house is empty. However, Abu Yusuf opines that if the house is occupied by a resident (e.g., tenant), the liability falls on the resident because he has more direct possession (yad). If there is no resident, then the owner becomes the one in possession and is liable. The passage also clarifies a condition: the liability of the owner's 'aqilah depends on the guardian of the deceased explicitly claiming that the owner committed the murder. If the claim is against someone else (e.g., a third party), the owner is not liable. This condition is cited from the Tatarkhaniyya. The passage thus shows the Hanafi school's nuanced approach: the qasamah is not automatic; it is tied to the claim and the person most directly responsible for the safety of the place.

Key Terms
اليَد (al-yad): Possession or control of property; the one with more specific yad is deemed primarily responsible for the premises.
العَاقِلَة (al-'āqilah): The group or tribe that pays blood-money on behalf of the liable individual, according to their inheritance shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, who bears the qasamah if a dead body is found in a house that is occupied by a tenant?
A) The owner of the house.
B) The tenant (resident).
C) Both owner and tenant equally.
D) The 'aqilah of the owner.

Key Takeaway
The owner is liable for qasamah only if the claim of murder is against him, and according to the majority, even if the house is empty.
